<leme no="1376">
<page sig="a2r">
<heading>
A 
COLLECTION
OF 
English WORDS 
Not Generally used, with their
Significations and Original, in two
Alphabetical Catalogues,

THE ONE
Of such as are proper to the Northern,
the other to the Southern Counties.

WITH
An Account of the preparing and
refining such Metals and Minerals
as are gotten in England.

The Second Edition, augmented with many
hundreds of Words, Observations, Letters, &amp;c.
By JOHN RAY: Fellow
of the Royal Society.

LONDON:
Printed for Christopher Wilkinson, at the
Black Boy over against S. Dunstan's 
Church in Fleetstreet. 1691.
</heading>
</page>
<page sig="a2v>
</page>
<page sig="a3r">

<section type="Dedication">

TO HIS 
Honoured FRIEND
Peter Courthope, Esq;
OF
DANNY in SUSSEX.

Sir,
THough I need no other
Motive to induce me
to present you with this
Collection of English Words,
but that I might take occasion
publickly to own my Obligati&shy;
ons to you, as well for your
</page>
<page sig="a3v">

long continued Friendship as for
the Assistance you have some&shy;
time afforded me in those Studies
to which I am I think naturally
inclined, yet one Circumstance
did more especially lead me to
make choice of you for its Pa&shy;
tron; and that is, that you were
the first that Contributed to it,
and indeed the Person that put
me upon it; and so it being in 
good measure your own, I have
reason to hope that you will
Favourably accept it. I confess
the Work is so inconsiderable,
that I am somewhat ashamed to
prefix your name before it, but
having nothing else left of my
own, which I design to trouble
the World with, as not knowing
</page>
<page sig="a4r">

whether I may live so long as to
perfect what I have now before
me; I chuse rather to present you
with this, than lose the Honour
of being known to have such a
Friend, or neglect the duty of
making acknowledgments where
they are due, especially having
already made Presents of this
Nature to others of my Friends,
which is enough to excuse this
Dedication intended to do other
purposes by

Sir,

Your very humble Servant,

JO. RAY.
</section>
</page>
<page sig="a4v">
</page>
<page sig="a5r">

<section type="Preface">
THE 
PREFACE.

Since the publishing this Collecti&shy;
on of Local Words, in the year 
1674. which were hastily ga&shy;
thered up by me, I received a 
Letter from my worthy Friend M. Fran&shy;
cis Brokesby, sometimes Fellow of Tri&shy;
nity College in Cambridge, and since 
Rector of Rowley in the East Riding 
of Yorkshire, attended with a large 
Catalogue of Northen Words, their 
significations, and Etymologies, to be 
added to a second Edition of this Col&shy;
lection, if ever it came to be reprinted; 
which then I did not expect that it 
would. But since it hath found so 
favourable acceptance among the inge&shy;
nious, that the former Impression be&shy;

</page>
<page sig="a5v">

ing disperst and exhausted, a new one 
is desired by the Bookseller concerned: 
I readily entertained the motion, that 
I might enrich my Book, and recom&shy;
mend it to the Reader by so consider&shy;
able an Addition, as also procure my 
Friend the praise due to his pains 
and performance. And lest I my self 
should defraud him, and intervert any 
part thereof, I hold my self obliged to 
advertise the Reader, that the greatest 
part of the Words added to the Nor&shy;
thern Collection are owing to him, 
though his name be not subjoyned. The 
rest are a supplement of such Words ob&shy;
served by the learned and ingenious, 
my honored and dear Friend Dr. Tan&shy;
cred Robinson as he found wanting in 
Mr. Brokesbyes Catalogne. The great&shy;
est part of the additional Words in the 
Southern Collection were contributed 
by my ingenious Friends Mr. Nicolas 
Jekyll of Sibble Heveningham, and 
Mr. Mansell Courtman Minister of 
Castle Heveningham in Essex. Since 
the Copy of this Collection was out of 

</page>
<page sig="a6r">

my hands, and delivered to the Book&shy;
seller in order to the Printing of it, I 
received three Catalogues of Local 
Words, two from my learned and wor&shy;
thily esteemed Friend Mr. Edward 
Lloyd of Oxford, one drawn up by 
himself, of British Words parallel to 
some of the Northern Words in this 
Collection, from which probably the 
Northern might be derived: the other 
communicated to him by Mr. Tomlin&shy;
son of Edmund Hall, a Cumberland 
Gentleman. The third from Mr. Wil&shy;
kinson a Bookseller in Fleetstreet, 
London, owner of the Copy of this 
Collection, sent him from Mr. Wil&shy;
liam Nicholson, an ingenious Mini&shy;
ster living in Cumberland. I found in 
it many Words already entred in my 
Collection, the most of which I thought 
fit to omit, though had they come time&shy;
ly enough they might have been useful 
to me, because they contain many pa&shy;
rallels in the Teutonick, Cimbrick, 
and old Gothick Languages, which 
might have been added in their places.

</page>
<page sig="a6v">

Some Words, I also observed therein of 
common and general use in most coun&shy;
tries of England, at least where I 
have lived or conversed, which I also 
omitted, (because it is not my design 
to write an English Glossary) but yet 
shall here mention them.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="benison(n)">Benison</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="benediction(n)"> for Benediction, which is 
not unusual among our Elegant Wri&shy;
ters.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bloom(n)">Blume,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="blossom(n)"> or Bloom for Blossome.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bower(n)">A Bowre,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bough(n)"> for an Arbour, because 
made of Bows, or as they usually spell 
it, Boughs of Trees: though I confess 
with us it is used neither for a House 
nor for a Room.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bridge(n)">A Brigge,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bridge(n)"> for a Bridge used at Cam&shy;
bridge. It is but a difference of Dia&shy;
lect.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="childermas day(n)">Childermas day,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="innocents day(n)"> for Innocents day.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="corse(n)">A Corse,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="dead body(n)"> for a dead body, which 
in my opinion is originally nothing but 
Corps.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="crag(n)">A Cragge,</form> <xpln lang="en"> probably from the British 
<term lang="br">Craig</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cun(v)" lexeme="con(v)">To Cun, or Con thanks:</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="give thanks(v)"> to give 
thanks.</xpln>
<lemenote> Sense of "cun(v)" and "con(v)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

</page>
<page sig="a7r">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="deft(a)">Deft,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="neat(a)" lexeme="pretty(a)"> for Neat, pretty.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fang(n)">Fangs,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="claw(n)"> for Claws Clutches, is a 
general Word.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fleer(v)">To Fleer</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="jeer(v)"> or Flyre, to laugh slily, to 
jeer.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gear(n)">Gear</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="clothes(n)" lexeme="gear(n)" lexeme="headgear(n)"> or Geer, for clothes, accou&shy;
trements, harness. So Women call the 
Linnen and what else they wear upon 
their Head, Head-gear: Gear is al&shy;
so used for trumpery, rubbish, so as 
stuffe is. Goodly gear.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="glede(n)">A Glead,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="kite(n)"> for a Kite, which he very 
probably deduces from gliding.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><editoraddition>Grave</editoraddition></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="grave(n)" lexeme="groove(n)">The Word Grave is not used in the 
South for digging wuth a Spade, but is 
appropriated to cutting upon Metal. 
But a Grave i.e. Sepulchrum is a 
Pit digged with a Spade, and we say 
a Spade-graft or a Spit deep. And a 
Groove is a Furrow made in Wood or 
Metal by Joyners, Smiths, or other 
Artificers.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="groat(n)">Groats,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="oatmeal(n)"> for great Oatmeal is a ge&shy;
neral Word.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gripe(n)">Gripe,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="groop(n)"> the same with Grupe, is 
frequently used with us for <term lang="la">fulcus, fos&shy;
sula, illex.</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page sig="a7v">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="harry the country(v)">Harrying the Country,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="waste(v)" lexeme="plunder(v)" lexeme="spoil(v)" lexeme="hen-harrier(n)"> is also ge&shy;
nerally used for wasting, plundering, 
spoiling it by any means. There is a 
sort of Puttock called a Hen-harrier 
from chasing, preying upon and destroy&shy;
ing of Poultry.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="than hie you(v)">Than Hie you,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hast you(v)"> for hast you, nothing 
wore common.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="lug(n)">Lugs,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ear(n)"> for Ears is a general but 
derisory Word. With Hair in Cha&shy;
racters and Lugs in text. Cleve&shy;
lands Poems.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="neb(n)">Neb,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bill(n)" lexeme="pen(n)" lexeme="vessel(n)"> is of frequent use, though not 
<emend err="for"></emend>for the Nose of a Man, yet for 
the Bill of a Bird, and metaphorically 
for the point of a Pen, or the long 
and slender Nose of any Vessel.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="nip(v)">To Nip,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="press between(v)"> for to press between the 
Fingers and Thumb not using the 
Nails, or with any Instrument that is 
flat as Tongs or the like: to press be&shy;
tween things that are edged, is called 
pinching.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="reek(n)">A Reek,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="steam(n)"> with us signifies not a 
Smoak, but a steam arising from any 
Liquor or moist thing heated.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page sig="a8r">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sad(a)">Sad,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="heavy(a)" lexeme="bread(n)"> is used also for heavy, spoken 
of Bread that rises not, or the like.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="strand(n)">A Strand,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="shore(n)" lexeme="strand(n)" lexeme="stranded(a)"> for a Shoar or bank of 
Sand, whence the Strand in Lon&shy;
don; and a Ship is said to be Strand&shy;
ed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="uncouth(a)">Uncouth,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="incongruous(a)"> is commonly used for ab&shy;
surb, incongruous.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ware(v)">Warre,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beware(v)"> for beware, as War Heads, 
or Horns.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wend(v)">Wented,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="acid(n)" lexeme="wort(n)"> for acid or a little changed, 
spoken of Wort.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="whittle(v)">To Whittle sticks,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="cut off the bark(v)" lexeme="whittle(n)"> to cut off the Bark 
with a Knife, to make them white. 
Hence also a Knife is in derision cal&shy;
led a Whittle.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wily(a)">Wilie,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="subtle(a)" lexeme="deceitful(a)"> Subtle, deceitful.</xpln></wordentry>

I was the less scrupulous of omit&shy;
ting these Words, because the Gentle&shy;
man himself intends to publish with a 
History of the Kingdom of North&shy;
umberland a large North-humbrick 
Glossary.

To these I might add some Words 
I observed in Mr. Hickes his Islan&shy;
dish Dictionary, by him noted for

</page>
<page sig="a8v">

Northern Words, v. <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Banne,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="curse(v)"> i.e. 
to Curse.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To make a Dinne,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="noise(n)"> i. e. a 
Noise, which we in Essex pronounce 
Dean, and is in frequent use.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">A Fang,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="claw(n)" lexeme="paw(n)"> 
for a Claw or Paw.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">A Frosh,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="frog(n)"> for a 
Frog.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Galts</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="sow(n)"> and Gelts, or as they 
here pronounce it Yelts, for young Sows 
before they have had their first Fare 
of Pigs.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Yell.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="howl(v)"> i.e. to cry out 
hideously, to howl.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Glow,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="be hot(v)"> i.e. to 
be hot.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Heave,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="lift up(v)"> i.e. to lift up.</xpln></wordentry> 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">The Huls of Corn</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chaff(n)">, i.e. the chaffe or 
covering from Hill to cover.</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To 
Lamme,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beat(v)"> i.e. to beat.</xpln></wordentry>

These Gentlemen being, I suppose 
North Country Men, and during their 
abode in the Universities or elsewhere, 
not happening to hear those Words used 
in the South, might suppose them to be 
proper to the North. The same error I 
committed my self in many Words that 
I put down for Southern, which after&shy;
wards I was advised were of use also 
in the North, viz. Arders, Auk and 
aukward, to Brimme, Bucksome, 
Chizzel, Clever, a Cob-iron, a Cot-&shy;

</page>
<page sig="a9r">

terel, to Cour down, to Cope, Crank, 
it Dares or Dears, a Dibble, a Dool, 
Feaberries, to Goyster, Hogs for 
Sheep, a Jarre, to Play, i.e. to Boyl, 
Shie, Temse-bread.

In the same Islandish Dictionary 
I find also some Northern Words not 
entred in my Catalogue, viz.

<term lang="nn">The Eand</term>, <term lang="la">Spiritus, &agrave; Cimbrico 
Ande.</term> To Byg, <term lang="la">&aelig;dificare</term>; Bigd <term lang="la">ha&shy;
bitatio</term>. To Britten Beef, to break 
the Bones of it. AS. <term lang="oe">Brittan</term> <term lang="la">fran&shy;
gere</term>. The Ey-brees, <term lang="la">Palpebr&aelig;</term> Ey&shy;
lids. Scot. <term lang="sc">Bran</term> <term lang="la">ab</term>Island, <term lang="ic">Brun</term>. We 
use Eybrows for <term lang="la">Supercilia</term>. To 
Dwine away, <term lang="la">Gradatim perire, inde</term> 
Dwindle <term lang="la">Dimin. &agrave;</term> Duyn <term lang="la">Islandico, 
Cesso, deficio</term>. Easles, Boreal. Isles, 
<term lang="la">Cinis ignitus, scintillans ab</term>Island. 
<term lang="ic">Eysa</term>. We in Essex use <term lang="ex">Easles</term> for the 
hot Embers, or as it were burning 
Coals of Straw only. A Fell, <term lang="la">mons</term>. 
Fournes fells, The Fell-foot. <term lang="la">ab
Islandico</term> <term lang="ic">Fel</term>, <term lang="la">Acclivitas</term>.

<term lang="la">Fliggurs Ebor.</term> Young Birds that 
can fly, fledge, Isl. <term lang="ic">Fleigur</term> <term lang="la">Volatilis.</term>

</page>
<page sig="a9v">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">The Gowk,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="cuckoo(n)"> the Cuckow, Island. 
<term lang="ic">Gaukur</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Helle water</form> <xpln lang="en">. <term lang="la">Effundere aquam.</term>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">A Whreak,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hawking(n)"> <term lang="la">Tussis</term>, a hauking, <term lang="la">Scre&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Ream,</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">manum ad aliquid capi&shy;

<term lang="la">Unguibus rapio</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Axel-tooth,</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Dens molaris</term>, Island

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">Yaud <term lang="la">Eboracensibus</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="horse(n)" lexeme="jade(n)"> a Horse, a 
Jade.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">To Lek,</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Stillo</term>, Island. <term lang="ic">Lek</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">The Fire lowes,</form> <xpln lang="en"> i. e. <term lang="la">flames Ebora&shy;
censibus</term>. Germ. <term lang="gr">Lohe</term>, <term lang="la">Flamma</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">The Munne,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="mouth(n)"> the Mouth. Island. 
<term lang="ic">Munnur</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

In Sir Tho. Browns eighth Tract,

</page>
<page sig="a10r">

which is of Languages, there are se&shy;
veral Words mentioned as of common 
use in Norfolk, or peculiar to the East-&shy;
Angle Countries, and not of general, 
viz. <term lang="nk">Bawnd, Bunny, Thurk, Enem&shy;
mis, Sammodithee, Mawther, Kedge, 
Seel, Straft, Clever, Matchly, Dere, 
Nicked, Stingy, Non eore, Feft, 
Thepes, Gosgood, Camp, Sibrit, 
Fangast, Sap, Cothish, Thokish, 
Bide owe, Paxwax.</term>

Of some of these the forementioned 
Mr. Hickes gives an account in the 
Preface to his Saxon Grammar, as 
Bunny, a swelling upon a stroke or 
blow on the Head or elsewhere, which 
he parallels with the Gotthick <term lang="go">Bango</term>
uteus, and the Islandick <term lang="ic">Ban</term>, a Wound, 
and <term lang="ic">Ben</term> vibex. We in Essex call it 
a <term lang="ex">Boine</term> on the Head. Bunny is also 
used as a flattering word <term lang="gk">&#x03CD;&pi;&omicron;&kappa;&omicron;&rho;&iota;&#x03DB;&iota;&shy;
&kappa;&#x03CC;&nu;</term> to Children. Bawnd tumens, as 
his Head is bownd, his head is swoln, 
from the forementioned Islandick word 
<term lang="ic">Bon</term>. Thurk or Thark is plainly 
from the Saxon <term lang="oe">deork</term>, dark <term lang="la">Enem&shy;</term>

</page>
<page sig="a10v">

<term lang="la">mis, n&egrave;, ne forte</term>, as Spar the door 
Enemmis he come, i. e. lest he come, he 
deduces probably from Eigenema or 
Einema an adverb of excluding or ex&shy;
cepting now in use among the Islanders. 
Sammodithu, a form of salutation sig&shy;
nifying, tell me how do you, pro&shy;
bably may be nothing but the Saxon 
<term lang="oe">s&aelig;g me hu dert &thorn;u</term>, rapidly pronounc'd, 
as we say Muchgooditte for Much 
good do it you. Mauther I take to 
be our Mothther a Girl or young Maid, 
of which I rather approve Sir Hen. 
Spelmans account, which see in my 
Collection. Seel, <term lang="la">Tempus</term>, entred in 
the Collection. Strast, <term lang="la">iratus, ir&acirc; excla&shy;
mans, Islandis</term> at Straffa <term lang="la">est objurgare, 
corripere, increpare</term>. Matchly; Per&shy;
fectly well. Islandis <term lang="ic">Maatlega, Magt&shy;
lega</term>; Sax. <term lang="oe">Mihtilice</term>, <term lang="la">vald&egrave;</term>, mightily. 
To Dere or Dare, entred in the Col&shy;
lection. Noneare, <term lang="la">mod&ograve;</term>. Isl. <term lang="ic">Nun&aelig;r</term>. 
[ere seems to signifie in old English be&shy;
fore as in Ere-now and in Erewhile, 
i.e. before now, before time, and ere 
I go, i.e, before I go, of which yore

</page>
<page sig="a11r">

seems to be but a Dialect, in days of 
yore. So non-ere may be not before, 
now] To Camp. To play at Foot&shy;
ball. Sax. <term lang="oe">Camp</term> is striving, and <term lang="oe">Cam&shy;
pian</term> to strive or contend. This word, 
for this exercise, extends over Essex 
as well as Norfolk and Suffolk. Si&shy;
brit is entred in the Collection. This 
Author makes it a compound of <term lang="me">Sib</term> and 
<term lang="oe">byrht</term> manifest. Angl. to Bruit, apud 
Salopienses to Brit, to Divulge and 
spread abroad: I should rather make 
it a compound of <term lang="me">Sib</term> and <term lang="la">ritus</term>. Fan&shy;
gast, a marriageable Maid, <term lang="la">viro ma&shy;
tura &amp; q. virum jam expetens</term>: per&shy;
chance from <term lang="oe">fengan</term> or <term lang="oe">fangan</term>, Sax. 
to take or catch, and <term lang="oe">aast</term> love, as 
much to say as taken with love or 
capable of love. To bide owe, <term lang="la">p&oelig;nas 
dare: unde constat</term> (saith he) bide <term lang="la">pro&shy;
fluxisse &agrave; Saxonico</term> <term lang="oe">wyte</term>, <term lang="la">quod p&oelig;nam 
mulctam, supplicium significant</term>. The 
other words which he leaves to others to 
give an account of are Kedge for brisk 
budge; clever, neat, elegant. See the 
Collection: to nick, to hit the time right

</page>
<page sig="a11v">

I nick'it, I came in the nick of time, 
just in time. Nick and Notch, i.e. 
<term lang="la">crena</term> are synonymous words, and to 
nick a thing seems to me to be origi&shy;
nally no more than to hit just the notch 
or mark, <term lang="la">scopum petere</term>: Stingy, 
pinching, sordid, narrow-spirited, I 
doubt whether it be of ancient use or 
Original, and rather think it to be a 
newly coined word. To feft, to per&shy;
suade, or indeavour to persuade. We 
in Essex use <term lang="ex">feffing</term> for putting, thrust&shy;
ing or obtruding a thing upon one, <term lang="la">do&shy;
num</term> or <term lang="la">Merces obtrudere</term>, but for 
the Etymon or Original I am to seek: 
Gosgood, i.e. Yeast or Barm, is no&shy;
thing but Gods-good, (<term lang="la">Bonum Di&shy;
vinum</term>) as they pronounce the word in 
Sussex and Kent, where it is in use; 
it is also called Beer-good. Thepes 
is the same with Febes or Feaberries, 
i.e. Goose-berries, a word used also in 
Cheshire, as Gerard witnesseth in his 
Herbal; but what Language it owes 
its Original to is further to be enquired. 
Cothish, Morose, and Thokish, sloth&shy;

</page>
<page sig="a12r">

ful, sluggish, I have no account to give 
of. Paxwax, for the Tendon or apo&shy;
neurosis to strengthen the Neck, and 
bind the Head to the Shoulders, I have 
nothing to say to, but that it is a word 
not confined to Norfolk or Suffolk, 
but far spread over England; used to 
my knowledge in Oxfordshire.

As for the Catalogues of English Birds 
and Fishes inserted in the first Editi&shy;
on of this Book, I thought fit to omit 
them in this; because they were very 
imperfect, and since much more fully 
given in the Histories of Birds and 
Fishes published by us; besides if God 
grant life and health I may put forth 
a particular methodical Synopsis of our 
English Animals and Fossills with Cha&shy;
racteristick Notes, and Observations 
upon them, which will swell to a con&shy;
siderable Volume, our Insects being more 
numerous than the Plants of this Island.

</section>

</page>
<page sig="a12v">
</page>

<page no="1">

<heading>
A
COLLECTION
OF
Local Words
Proper to the
NORTH and SOUTH Countries.

</heading>

<section type="Glossary">

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A"><alpha>A.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="addle(v)">TO Adle</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="earn(v)"> or Addle; to Earn, from 
the ancient Saxon word <term lang="oe">Ed-lean</term>, 
a reward, r<emend err="c">ec</emend>ompence or re&shy;
quital.</xpln></wordentry>
      
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aftermath(n)">After-math;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pasture(n)" lexeme="roughings(n)"> the Pasture after the Grass 
hath been mowed. In other places called 
Roughins.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="agate(adv)"><term lang="ch">Agate;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="go(v)" lexeme="way(n)"> Ches. Just going, as I am A&shy;
gate. Gate in the Northern Dialects signi&shy;

</page>
<page no="2">

fies a way, so that a Agate is at or upon the 
way.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="alantom(adv)">Alantom;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="distance(n)"> At a distance.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="amel(pp)">A mell;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="among(pp)" lexeme="betwixt(pp)"> Among, betwixt, contracted 
from a middle; or perchance from the 
French word <term lang="fr">Mesler</term>, signifying to mingle, 
whence our English Medley is derived. 
Some pronounce it ameld.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="a'nauntrins(adv)">Anauntrins;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="if so be(v)"> If so be. I know not what 
the Original of this should be, unless it be 
from An, for if, and Auntrins contracted 
from Peradventure.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="anent(pp)">Anent;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="over against(pp)"> Over against, concerning. A 
word of frequent use among the Scots. 
Some deduce it from the Greek <term lang="gk">&#x03F2;&nu;&alpha;&nu;&tau;&#x03AF;, &#x03F2;&nu;&alpha;&nu;&tau;&#x03AF;&omicron;&nu;</term>
<term lang="la">Oppositum. Nec mal&egrave; san&egrave; (inquit</term> Skinne&shy;
rus in <term lang="la">Etymologico Linguae Anglicanae) si 
vel soni vel sensus convenientiam respicias. 
Sed quo commercio Gr&aelig;ci Scotis totius Eu&shy;
rop&aelig; longitudine dissitis vocabula impertiri po&shy;
tuerunt? Mallem igitur deducere ab</term> AS 
<term lang="oe">Nean</term> <term lang="la">Prope, addit&acirc; particul&acirc; initiali otio&shy;
sa A</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arain(n)"><term lang="nt">An Arain;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="spider(n)"> a Spider: <term lang="la">&agrave; Lat. Aranea</term>. It 
is used only for the larger kind of Spiders. 
Nottinghamshire.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arf(a)">Arf;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="afraid(a)"> Afraid.</xpln>
<lemenote> "arf(a)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ark(n)">An Ark;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chest"> A large Chest to put Corn or 
Fruit in like the Bing of a Buttery; from 
the Latin word <term lang="la">Arca</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="3">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arles(n)">Arles or Earles;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="earnest(a)" lexeme="earnest penny(n)">  Earnest, an Arles-penny, 
an Earnest-penny, from the Latin Word 
<term lang="la">arrha</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arr(n)">An Arr;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="scar(n)" lexeme="pock-arr(n)"> A Skar. Pock-arrs, the Marks 
made by the Small Pox. This is a gene&shy;
ral Word, common both to North and 
South.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="arvill-supper(n)">Arvill-Supper;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="feast(n)"> A Feast made at Fune&shy;
rals: in part still retained in the North.</xpln>
<lemenote> "arvill-supper(n)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="asker(n)">An Aiker;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="newt(n)" lexeme="eft(n)"> a Newt, or Eft, <term lang="la">Salamandra 
aquatica</term>.</xpln>
<lemenote> "aiker(n)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="astite(adv)">Astite;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="shortly(a)" lexeme="as soon(a)" lexeme="tide(n)" lexeme="anon(adv)"> Anon. shortly, or as soon. i.e. 
<term lang="nn">As Tide, Tide</term> in the North signifies soon, 
and <term lang="nn">tider</term> or <term lang="nn">titter</term> sooner. The tider (that 
is the sooner) you come, the tider you'll go; 
from the Saxon <term lang="oe">Tid</term>, signifying time, which 
is still in use, as in Shrove-tide, Whitsun&shy;
tide, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="easily(adv)">As Asly;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="willingly(adv)"> As willingly.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="attercop(n)"><term lang="cu">An Attercob;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="spider's web(n)"> A Spiders Web. Cum&shy;
berland.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="auld-farrand(a)">Aud-farand;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="grave(a)" lexeme="witty(a)"> Children are said to be so, 
when grave or witty, beyond what is usual 
in such as are of that age.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="auld(a)">Aud;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="old(a)"> Old. Var. Dial. as Caud for Cold, 
Wauds for Wolds, Aum for Elm. And Fa&shy;
rand the Humor or Genius, <term lang="la">Ingenium</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="average(n)">Average;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="roughings(n)" lexeme="average(a)" lexeme="aver(n)" lexeme="haver(n)"> The breaking of Corn Fields; 
Eddish, Roughings. Average in Law 
signifies either the Beasts which Tenants 

</page>
<page no="4">

and Vassals were to provide their Lords 
for certain Services, or that Mony that 
was laid out by Merchants to repair the 
Losses suffered by Shipwrack; and so it is 
deduced from the old word Aver [<term lang="la">Averi&shy;
um</term>] signifying a laboring Beast: or <term lang="la">Ave&shy;
ria</term> signifying Goods or Chattels, from the 
French <term lang="fr">Avoir</term> to have or possess. But in 
the sense we have used it, it may possibly 
come from Haver signifying Oats; or 
from <term lang="la">Averia</term>, Beasts, being as much as 
Feeding for Cattle, Pasturage.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aum(n)">Aum;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="elm(n)"> Elm. Var. Dial.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ambry(n)" lexeme="aumbry(n)">An Aumbry, or Ambry, or Aumery.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pantry(n)" lexeme="cupboard(n)"> A 
Pantry or Cupboard to set Victuals in: 
Skinner makes it to signifie a Cupboard's 
Head, or Side-Table: <term lang="la">super quam vasa 
mensaria &amp; tota argentea supellex ad usum 
conviviorum exponitur: &agrave;</term> Fr. G. <term lang="fr">Aumoire, 
Armaire, &amp; Armoire</term>, It. <term lang="it">Armaro</term> <term lang="la">idem sig&shy;
nantibus, q. d. Latin&egrave; Armarium</term>. Prov. 
No sooner up, but the head in the Aumbry, 
and Nose in the Cup. In which Sentence 
it must needs signifie a Cupboard for Vi&shy;
ctuals.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aund(a)">Aund;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ordained(a)"> Ordained: <term lang="la">forsan per contractio&shy;
nem.</term> I am aun'd to this luck, i.e. Ordain'd.</xpln>
<lemenote> "aund(a)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="aunters(adv)">Aunters;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="peradventure(n)"> Peradventure, or, in case, if 
it chance. I guess it to be contracted from 
Adventure, which was first mollified into 

</page>
<page no="5">

Auventure, and then easily contracted in&shy;
to Aunter. It signifies also needless Scru&shy;
ples, in that usual Phrase, He is troubled 
with Aunters.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="undern(n)">The Aunder, or as they pronounce it in 
Cheshire, <term lang="ch">Oneder</term>;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="afternoon(n)"> The Afternoon.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="awn(n)">Awns;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="wheat(n)" lexeme="barley(n)"> <term lang="la">Arist&aelig;</term>, The Beards of Wheat or 
Barley. In Essex they pronounce it <term lang="ex">Ails</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

</wordgroup1>
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="B"><alpha>B.</alpha> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="backstex(n)">A Backstex;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="baker(n)"> A Baker.</xpln>
<lemenote> "backstex(n)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="badger(n)">A Badger;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="buy corn(v)"> Such as buy Corn, or 
other Commodities in one place, and 
carry them to another. It is a Word of 
general use.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bain(a)">Bain;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="willing(a)" lexeme="forward(a)"> Willing, Forward: opposed to 
Lither.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="balk(n)">The Balk or Bawk;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="summer-beam(n)" lexeme="dorman(n)" lexeme="pole(n)" lexeme="balk(nv)"> The Summer&shy;
beam, or Dorman, Balks, Bawks: Poles 
laid over a Stable or other Building for the 
Roof, <term lang="la">&agrave; Belgico, &amp; Teuton.</term> Balk, <term lang="la">Trabs, 
tignum</term>. In common speech a Balk is the 
same with <term lang="la">Scamnum</term> in Latin, i.e. a piece 
of Land which is either casually over&shy;
slip'd, and not turned up in plowing, or 
industriously left untouched by the Plow, 
for a Boundary between Lands, or some 
other use. Hence to balk, is frequently

</page>
<page no="6">

used metaphorically, for to pass over.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="balk-staff(n)">A Balk staff;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="quarterstaff(n)"> A Quarter-staff, a great 
Staff like a Pole or Beam.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bannock(n)">A Bannock;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="oat-cake(n)" lexeme="tharf-cake(n)" lexeme="clap-bread(n)" lexeme="kitchiness-bread(n)" lexeme="riddle cake(n)" lexeme="jannock(n)"> An Oat-cake kneaded with 
Water only and baked in the Embers. In 
Lancashire, and other parts of the North, 
they make several sorts of Oaten bread, 
which they call by several Names: as 1. 
Tharcakes, the same with Bannocks, viz. 
Cakes made of Oat-meal, as it comes from 
the Mill and fair Water, without Yeast 
or Leaven, and so baked. 2. Clap-bread: 
Thin hard Oat-cakes. 3. Kitchiness-bread: 
Thin soft Oat-cakes made of thin Batter. 
4. Riddle-cakes: thick Sour-cakes, from 
which differs little that which they call 
Hand-hoven Bread, having but little Lea&shy;
ven, and being kneaded stiffer. 5. Jan&shy;
nock, Oaten bread made up in Loaves.</xpln>
<lemenote> "kitchiness-bread(n)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bargh(n)"><term lang="yk">A Bargh;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="horse-way(n)"> A Horseway up a steep Hill, 
York-shire.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bairn(n)">A Barn or Bearn;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="child(n)"> A Child. It is an an&shy;
cient Saxon word. In the ancient Teu&shy;
tonick, <term lang="tt">Barn</term> signifies a Son, derived per&shy;
chance from the Syriack <term lang="sy">Bar</term>, <term lang="la">Filius</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bar(n)"><term lang="yk">A Barr;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gate(n)"> A Gate of a City. York. As 
Bootham Bar, Monk-bar, Michael Gate-Bar 
in the City of York.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bawaty(n)">Bawaty</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="linsey-woolsey(n)"> or Bowety; Linsey-Wolsey.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bairn(n)" lexeme="team(n)" lexeme="bairn-team(n)">Bearn-teams;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="children(n)" lexeme="teeming woman(n)"> Broods of Children, as 

</page>
<page no="7">

they expounded it to me. I find that 
<term lang="oe">Bearn-team</term> in the Saxon, signifies Issue, 
Offspring, Children, from <term lang="oe">team</term>, soboles, and 
<term lang="oe">Bearn</term>. A teeming Woman is still in use 
for one that is apt to bear Children.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bearing(n)" lexeme="with child(n)"><term lang="yk">Bea<emend err="t">r</emend>ing with Child;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="breeding(a)"> Breeding, gravid. 
York-shire.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beck(n)">A Beck;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="brook(n)"> a small Brook. A Word com&shy;
mon to the ancient Saxon, High and Low 
Dutch and Danish. Hence the Termina&shy;
tions of many Towns, Sand beck, Well&shy;
beck, &amp;.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bield(n)">Beeld;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="shelter(n)"> Shelter.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="birr(n)">Beer or Birre; q. Beare;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="force(n)" lexeme="might(n)"> Force, might. 
<term lang="ch">With aw my Beer.</term> Cheshire. i.e. With all 
my Force.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bight(n)">Beight of the Elbow;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bend of the elbow(n)"> Bending of the 
Elbow. Chesh. A Substantive from the 
Preterperfect Tense of Bend, as Bought of 
the like signification, from Bow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="belive(adv)">Belive;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="anon(adv)" lexeme="by and by(adv)" lexeme="towards night(adv)"> Anon, by and by, or towards 
night. By the Eve. This mollifying the 
into le being frequent in the North, as to 
la for to the. We have the Word in Chau&shy;
cer for Anon.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bensel(v)">To bensel;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bang(v)" lexeme="beat(v)"> To bang or beat. <term lang="la">Vox Ru&shy;
stica Ebor.</term>.</xpln></wordentry>
	
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="berry(v)">To Berry;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="thresh(v)"> To thresh, i.e. to beat out 
the Berry, or Grain of the Corn. Hence 
a Berrier, a Thresher: and the Berrying&shy;
stead, the Threshing Floor.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="8">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bid(v)">To Bid</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pray(v)" lexeme="bedesman(n)" lexeme="bead(n)"> or Bede; To pray. Hence a 
Bedes man, one that prays for others: and 
those little Globules, with which they 
number their Prayers, are called Bedes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="biggening(n)">Biggening:</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="biggen(v)"> I wish you a good biggen&shy;
ing: i.e. A good getting up again after ly&shy;
ing in. <term lang="la">Votum pro puerpera.</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="birch(n)">A Birk;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="birch-tree(n)"> A Birch-Tree. Var. Dial.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bisson(a)">Bizen'd;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="blind(a)"> Skinner writes it Beesen or Bee&shy;
zen or Bison: Blinded. From By signify&shy;
ing besides, and the Dutch word <term lang="du">Sin</term>, sig&shy;
nifying Sense. q. d. <term lang="la">Sensu omnium nobilissi&shy;
mo orbatus</term>: saith he.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blake(a)">Blake;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="yellow(a)"> Yellow, spoken of Butter and 
Cheese. As blake as a Paigle.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cow-blake(n)">Cow-blakes:</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="casing(n)"> Casings, Cow-dung dried, 
used for Fewel.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bleb(n)">A Bleb;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="blister(n)" lexeme="bain(n)" lexeme="bubble(n)"> a Blister, a Bain, also a Bub&shy;
ble in the Water.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bleed well(v)">Corn Bleeds well;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="thresh(v)"> when upon threshing 
it yields well.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blate(a)">Bleit or Blate;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bashful(a)"> Bashful. <term lang="sc">A toom purse 
makes a bleit Merchant.</term> Scot. Prov. That 
is, an empty Purse makes a shamefac'd 
Merchant. <term lang="la">Fortasse</term> q. Bleak or blank.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="browden(a)"><term lang="ch">Bloten;</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="ch">Fond</term>, as Children are of their 
Nurses. Chesh.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blow-milk(n)">Blow-Milk;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="skimmed milk(n)"> Skim'd or floten Milk; 
from whence the Cream is blown off.</xpln>
<lemenote> "blow-milk(n)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bluff(v)">To Bluffe;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="blindfold(v)"> To blind-fold.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="9">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="blush(v)">To blush another;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="blush(n)"> to be like him in 
Countenance. In all Countries we say, 
He of she hath a Blush of; i.e. resembles 
such another.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="body(n)"><term lang="yk">A Body;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="simpleton(n)"> A Simpleton. Yorkshire.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="boke(v)"><term lang="ch">To Boke at one:</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> To point at one. Chesh. 
i.e. to poke at one.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bolk(v)">To Boke;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="nauseate(v)" lexeme="vomit(v)" lexeme="belch(v)"> To nauseate, to be ready to 
vomit, also to belch. <term lang="la">Vox agro Lincolni&shy;
ensi familiaris (inquit Skinnerus) Alludit 
saltem Hispan. Bossar vomere, Boquear, osci&shy;
tare seu Pandiculari; vel possit deflects &agrave; La&shy;
tino evocare, vel melius &agrave;</term> Belg. <term lang="bl">Boocken</term>, 
Boken <term lang="la">pulsare, vel</term> Fuycken <term lang="la">Trudere, pro&shy;
trudere. Vomitus enim est rerum vomitu re&shy;
jectarum qu&aelig;dam protrusio seu extrusio.</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cleam, cleme(v)"><term lang="li">To Cleam;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="glue together(v)" lexeme="cleam(v)" > A Word of frequent use in

fasten with Glue. <term lang="la">ab</term> AS. <term lang="oe">Cl&aelig;mian, be&shy;
cl&aelig;mian</term>. <term lang="la">Oblinere, unde nostrum</term> clammy. 
AS. <term lang="oe">clam</term>, <term lang="la">Plasma, emplastrum</term>: Danic. 
<term lang="da">Kliiner</term>, <term lang="la">Glutino. Nescio autem an verbum</term> 
<term lang="oe">cl&aelig;mian</term> &amp; <term lang="la">Nom.</term> <term lang="oe">Clam</term> <term lang="la">orta sint &agrave; Lat. Li&shy;
mus, Limus enim propter lentorem admotis 
corporibus adh&aelig;ret,</term> Skinner. In Yorkshire, 

</page>
<page no="15">

to <term lang="yk">cleame</term> or <term lang="yk">clame</term> is to spread thick: As 
he <term lang="yk">cleam'd</term> Butter on his Bread; the Co&shy;
lors are laid on as if they were <term lang="yk">clamed</term> on 
with a Trowel, spoken of Colors ill laid 
on in a Picture.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="clemmed(a)">Clem'd or clam'd;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="starved(a)" lexeme="thirsty(a)"> Starved, because by 
Famine the Guts and Bowels are as it were 
clammed or stuck together. Sometimes it 
signifies thirsty, and we know in thirst the 
mouth is very often clammy.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="clough(n)"><term lang="oe">A Clough;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="valley(n)"> a Valley between two steep

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="clumse(a)"><term lang="li">Clumps,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="idle(a)" lexeme="lazy(a)" lexeme="unhandy(a)" lexeme="clumsy(a)" lexeme="lumpish(a)"> Clumpst; Idle, lazy, unhandy,

&agrave;</term> Belg. <term lang="bl">Klonte, Klonter</term>, <term lang="la">vel potius</term> 
<term lang="tt">Klompe</term>, Teut. <term lang="tt">Klamp</term>, <term lang="ch">Massa, q.d. Car&shy;
nis massa, spiritus &amp; ingenii expers, vel &agrave;</term>
Belg. <term lang="bl">Lompsch</term>, <term lang="la">stupidus, piger, hoc fort. &agrave; 
Lompe, Clompe massa ob rationem jam 
dictam: vel fort&egrave; clumps contr. &amp; corr. &agrave;
nostro</term> clownish. Skinner. This is, I sup&shy;
pose, the same with our clumsy, in the 
South, signifying unhandy, clumpst with 
Cold, i.e. benummed: or it may be from

<term lang="la">massa</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="coop(n)">A Coop,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="muck-coop(n)" lexeme="lime-coop(n)"> a Muck-coop, a Lime-coop; a

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="daysman(n)">A Dayes man;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="arbitrator(n)" lexeme="umpire(n)" lexeme="judge(n)" lexeme="day(n)"> an Arbitrator; an Um&shy;

&theta;&rho;&alpha;&pi;&#x03AF;&nu;&chi; &#x03AE;&mu;&#x03AF;&rho;&alpha;</term> mans day, I Cor. 3.13. is

Prov. From the Saxon <term lang="oe">F&aelig;gan</term>, <term lang="la">L&aelig;&shy;
tus, hilaris</term>, <term lang="oe">F&aelig;gnian</term>, <term lang="la">gaudere</term>. Psalm Ixxi. 
21. In the Translation of our Liturgy: 
My lips will be fain when I sing unto thee.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="phantom corn(a)">Fantome Corn;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="light corn(n)" lexeme="phantom(a)" lexeme="phantom(n)"> lank or light Corn: 
Fantome Flesh: when it hangs loose on the 
Bone. A Fantome, a conceited person.

</page>
<page no="25">

The French call a Spirit, appearing by 
night, or a Ghost, a <term lang="fr">Fantosme</term>, from 
Phantasma, Spectrum. So then Phantosme 
Corn is Corn that hath as little bulk or so&shy;
lidity in it as a Spirit or Spectre.
</xpln><lemenote> Sense of "phantom(n)" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="farrand(n)">Farand</form> <xpln lang="en"> is used in composition: as Fight&shy;
ing-Farand, i.e. in a fighting humor. 
V. Aud farand.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="farrantly(a)">Farantly;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="farantly(a)"> handsom. Fair and farantly:

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gool(n)"><term lang="li">A Gool;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ditch(n)" lexeme="gulley(n)" lexeme="gullet(n)"> a Ditch, Lincolnsh: <term lang="la">Lacuna,

&agrave;</term> Fr. G. <term lang="gr">Jaule</term>, <term lang="of">Gaiole</term>, <term lang="la">Latin&egrave; Caveola,

hujusmodi incidimus, e&acirc; tanquam cavea aut 
carcere detinemur</term>, &amp;c. Skin. Hence a 
Gully and Gullet, a little Ditch; and Gullet 
the Throat: or rather from the Latin <term lang="la">Gu&shy;
la,</term>, from whence perchance Gool it self 
may be derived.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="low(n)">A Loe:</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hill(n)" lexeme="hillock(n)" lexeme="heap(n)" lexeme="tumulus(n)" lexeme="burial mound(n)"> a little round hill, a great 
heap of stones: <term lang="la">ab</term> AS <term lang="oe">L&aelig;we</term>, <term lang="la">Agger, a&aelig;
cervus, cumulus, tumulus</term>, a Law, Low, 
Loo or high ground, not suddenly rising 
as an hill, but by little and little, tillable 
also, and without Wood. Hence that 
name given to many Hillocks and Heaps 
of Earth, to be found in all parts of 
England, being no other but so much 
congested Earth, brought in a way of 
Burial used of the Ancients, thrown up&shy;
on the Bodies of the dead. Somner in 
Diction. Saxon.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="neap(n)">A Nape or Neap;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="wood(n)"> a piece of Wood,

ancient Romans thus described by Plutarch,
<term lang="gk">&xi;&#x03CD;&lambda;&omicron;&nu; &delta;&iota;&pi;&lambda;&omicron;&upsilon;  &#x03CC; &tau;&alpha;&iota;&sigmaf; &#x03AC;&mu;&#x03AC;&xi;&alpha;&iota;&sigmaf; &#x03CC;&phi;&iota;&sigmaf;&#x03AC;&sigma;&iota;</term>, which <term lang="la">Is.

tero extremo in duo cornua, quod subjicitur 
temoni plaustri, quoties volunt aurig&aelig; rectum 
stare plaustrum oneratum. Furca</term> was used 
in several kinds of punishments. <term lang="la">V. Casau&shy;
bon. ibid.</term> pag. 443. Edit. Fraconf.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="nesh(a)">Nash or Nesh;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="washy(a)" lexeme="tender(a)" lexeme="weak(a)" "puling(a)" lexeme="nice(a)" lexeme="soft(a)" lexeme="delicate(a)" lexeme="effeminate(a)" lexeme="tame(a)" lexeme="gentle(a)" lexeme="mild(a)" lexeme="nescook(n)" lexeme="tenderling(n)"> Washy, tender, weak, 
puling. Skinner makes it proper to Wor&shy;
cestershire, and to be the same in Sence 
and Original with Nice. But I am sure 
it is used in many other Counties, I be&shy;
lieve all over the North-West part of 
England, and also in the midland, as in 
Warwickshire. As for the Etymology of 
it, it is doubtless no other then the ancient 
Saxon word <term lang="oe">Nesc</term>, signifying soft, tender, 
delicate, effeminate, tame, gentle, mild.

</page>
<page no="52">

Hence our Nescook in the same Sence, i.e. 
a tenderling, Somner.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="poke(n)">A Poke;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="sack(n)" lexeme="bag(n)" lexeme="pocket(n)" lexeme="poke(n)"> a Sack or Bag. It is a general 
word in this Sense all over England, 
though mostly used ludicrously, as are 
Gang, and Keal, &amp;c. because borrowed 
of the Northern People. Hence Pocket, 
a little Poke: and the Proverbs, to buy a 
Pig in a Poke, and when the Pig is profer'd 
hold ope the Poke. Mr. Brokesby informs 
me, that with them in the East Riding of 
Yorkshire, the word <term lang="yk">Sack</term> is appropriated 
to a Poke that holds four Bushels: and 
that Poke is a general word for all Mea&shy;
sures; hence a Met-Poke, a 3 Bushel 
Poke, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="staddle(n)">A Staddle;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="mark(n)" lexeme="impression(n)" lexeme="staddle(n)"> a mark or impression made

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="stang(n)">A Stang;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bar(n)" lexeme="stang(v)" lexeme="ride(v)" lexeme="cowl-staff(n)" lexeme="acre(n)" lexeme="rood(n)"> a wooden Bar; <term lang="la">ab</term> AS. <term lang="oe">stang</term>,

<term lang="la">Pertica, sed nostro fonte haustum</term>. This 
word is still used in some Colleges in the 
University of Cambridge; to stang Scho&shy;
lars in Christmas, being to cause them 
to ride on a Colt-Staff or Pole, for mis&shy;
sing of Chappel. It is used likewise 
here [in the East Riding of Yorkshire] 
for the fourth part of an Acre, a Rood. 
Mr. Brokesby.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="twill(n)">A Twill;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="spool(n)" lexeme="quill(n)" lexeme="pen(n)" lexeme="reed(n)"> a Spoole: from Quill. In 
the South they call it winding of Quills, 
because anciently, I suppose, they wound 
the Yarn upon Quills for the Weavers, 
though now they use Reeds. Or else 
Reeds were called Quills, as in Latin <term lang="la">ca&shy;
lami</term>. For Quills, or Shafts of Birds 
Feathers, are now called <term lang="la">calami</term>, because 
they are employed for the same use of 
writing, which of old Reeds only were, 
and to this day are in some parts of the 
World. The word Pen, now used for 
the Instrument we Write with, is no other 
than the Latin <term lang="la">Penna</term>, which signifies the 
Quill or hard Feather of any Bird, and 
is a very proper word for it, because our 
Pens are now made of such Quills, which 
(as I said) were anciently made of Reeds.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fare(n)">A Fare of Pigs;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="farrow(v)" lexeme="yean(v)" lexeme="calve(v)" lexeme="foal(v)" lexeme="whelp(v)"> is so many as a Sow 
bringeth forth at one time. To farrow 
is a word peculiar to a Sows bringing 
forth Pigs. Our Language abounds in 
unnecessary words of this and other 
kinds. So a Sheep is said to Yean, a Cow
to Calve, a Mare to Foal, a Bitch to 
Whelp, &amp;c. All which words signifie no 
more than <term lang="la">Parere</term>, to bring forth. So for 
Sexes we have the like superfluous words,

</page>
<page no="98">

as Horse and Mare, Bull and Cow, Ram 
and Sheep, Dog and Bitch, Boar and 
Sow, &amp;c. Whereas the Difference of 
Sex were better signified by a Termi&shy;
nation.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="feaberry(n)"><term lang="sk">Feabes or Feaberries;</term><term lang="le"></term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gooseberry(n)" lexeme="thebe(n)"> Gooseberries, Suff.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gray of the morning(n)">The Gray of the Morning;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="daybreak(n)"> Break of

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="ex">A Modher or Modder, Mothther;</term><term lang="nk"></term><term lang="sk"></term><term lang="ca"></term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="girl(n)" lexeme="wench(n)"> a Girl 
or young Wench: used all over the Eastern 
Part of England, v.g. Essex, Suff. Norf. 
Cambr. From the ancient Danish word 
<term lang="da">Moer</term>, <term lang="la">Quomodo</term> (saith Sir H. Spelman in 
<term lang="la">Glossario) &agrave; Danis oriundi Norfolcienses pu&shy;

</page>
<page no="107">

ellam hodie vocant, quod interea rident An&shy;
gli preteri, vocis nescientes probitatem. Cupio 
patrio meo suffragari idiomati. Intelligen&shy;
dum igitur est Norfolciam hanc nostram (qu&aelig; 
inter alios aliquot Angli&aelig; Comitatus in Da&shy;
norum transiit ditionem, An. Dom. 876.) 
Danis maxim&egrave; habitatam fuisse, eor&uacute;mque 
legibus, lingua atque moribus imb&ugrave;tam. Claras 
illi virgines &amp; puellas (ut Arcto&aelig; gentes 
ali&aelig;) <term lang="da">Moer</term> appellabant. Inde qu&aelig; canen&shy;
do heroum laudes &amp; poemata palmam retu&shy;
lere (teste Olao Wormio) <term lang="da">Scaldmoer</term>, i.e. 
Virgines cantatrices: qu&aelig; in pr&aelig;liis glori&shy;
am ex fortitudine sunt adept&aelig; <term lang="da">Sciold Moer</term> 
hoc est Scutiferas virgines nuncup&acirc;runt. Eo&shy;
dem nomine ips&aelig;, Amazones, &amp;c. En quan&shy;
tum in spreta jam voce antiqu&aelig; glori&aelig;. Sed 
corrumpi hanc fateor vulgari labio, quod</term> Mo&shy;
ther <term lang="la">matrem significans etiam pro</term> <term lang="da">Moer</term>, 
h.e. <term lang="la">puella pronunciat</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="saunter(v)">To Santer about;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="idle(v)" lexeme="loiter(v)"> or go Santering up 
and down. It is derived from 
<term lang="la">Saincte terre</term>, i.e. The Holy Land, be&shy;
cause of old time when there were fre&shy;
quent Expeditions thither: many idle per&shy;
sons went from place to place, upon pre&shy;
tence that they had taken, or intended 
to take the Cross upon them, and to go 
thither. It signifies to idle up and down, 
to go loitering account.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="say(n)"><term lang="sk">Say of it;</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="taste(n)" lexeme="assay(v)" lexeme="try(v)" lexeme="prove(v)" lexeme="attempt(v)"> i.e. tast of it, Suff. Say for 
Assay, <term lang="la">per Aph&aelig;resin</term>, Assay from the French 
<term lang="fr">essayer</term>, and the Italian <term lang="it">assaggiare</term>, to try, 
or prove, or attempt; all from the Latin 
word <term lang="la">sapio</term>, which signifies also to taste.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="yepsen(n)" lexeme="gowpen(n)"><term lang="ex">A Yaspen or Yeepsen;</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> in Essex signifies 
as much as can be taken up in both 
Hands joyn'd together. Gouldman ren&shy;
ders it <term lang="la">vola seu manipulus, fortean &agrave; nostro.</term> 
Grasping, <term lang="la">elis&acirc; propter euphoniam liter&acirc; 
canin&acirc; r, and g, in y facillim&acirc; san&egrave; &amp; vul&shy;
gatissima nostr&aelig; lingu&aelig; mutatione transeunte: 
q.d. quantum quis vola comprehendere potest</term>, 
Skinner.</xpln></wordentry>

In Sussex for hasp, clasp, wasp, they 
pronounce hapse, clapse, wapse, &amp;c. for 
neck nick, for throat throtte, for choak, 
chock. Set'n down, let'n stand, come 
again and fet'n anon. C'have cat so much 
c'ham quit a quot, Devon. i.e. I can eat 
no more, I have eat so much that I am 
cloyed.

<heading>
A Catalogue of Local Words 
parallel'd with British or Welsh, 
by my learned and ingenious 
Friend Mr. Edward Lloyd of 
Oxford.

The Syllables thus mark'd ^ are long 
thus ' very short and smart.
</heading>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ark(n)">1. [English] AN Ark;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chest(n)"> a 
large Chest for Corn.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">1. [British] Arkh,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="coffin(n)"> Lat. 
<term lang="la">Arca, cista</term>. But the modern sig&shy;
nification is a Cof&shy;
fin. It is doubtless 
of the same origin 
with the Latin 
word, though we 
cannot say that all 
that are so have 
been borrowed from 
the Romans.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="attercrop(n)">2. [English] An Attercop;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="spider's web(n)"> 
A Spiders Web. 
Mr. Nicolson gives 
the Etymology of 
this word from the 
Saxon. I rather 
think it originally 
British, because re&shy;
maining in use only 
in Cumberland.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">2. [British] Cop,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="spider(n)"> and Cop&shy;
pin is Spider; but 
a Spiders web we 
call <term lang="br">gw&ecirc;r-cop</term> and 
corruptly <term lang="br">Gwydyr goppyn</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

now grown obso&shy;
lete. I suppose we

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="helo(a)">24. [English] Helo;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bashful(a)"> Bash&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">24. [British] Gw^yl,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bashful(a)"> Bash&shy;

<term lang="br">w^yl</term>, as <term lang="br">Merkhw^yl</term>,

</page>

<term lang="br">m&agrave;e yn w^yl</term>, He is

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="knoll(n)">25. [English] Knoll;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hill(n)"> a lit&shy;

or cop of a Hill or

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">25. [British] Klol,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="head(n)"> the 
Head. The Hills in 
Wales are generally 
denominated by 
metaphors from 
some parts of the 
body. Ex. gr. <term lang="br">Pen 
maenmawr, y B&egrave;n&shy;
glog, Tal y' Lhykhay, 
Ker'n y Bw^kh, y 
vr&ograve;n d&ecirc;g, Kev'n y 
Braikh, y Grimmong</term>. 
<term lang="br">Pen</term> signifying a 
Head, <term lang="br">Penglog</term> a 
Skull; <term lang="br">T&aacute;l</term> the fore&shy;
head; <term lang="br">Kern</term> one side 
of the Face <term lang="br">y Vron</term> 
the Breast; <term lang="br">Keven</term> 
the Back. <term lang="br">Braikh</term> 
an Arm, and <term lang="br">Krim&shy;
mog</term> a Leg.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ware(v)">29. [English] To Ware ones

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">29. [British] Gwarrio,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="spend money(v)"> to

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="yule(n)">30. [English] Yule;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="christmas(n)"> Christ&shy;
mass, Fr. Junius (<term lang="la">in 
Lexico suo</term> AS.) <term lang="la">vo&shy;
cem Zehul factum 
putat</term> <term lang="gk">&#x03F0;&alpha;&tau;&#x0384; &#x03AD;&xi;&omicron;&chi;&#x03AE;&nu;</term> <term lang="la">&agrave; 
Britan</term>. <term lang="br">Gwyl</term>, <term lang="la">Fe&shy;
stum Fevi&aelig; Mr. Ni&shy;
colson</term>. So that Yule 
is originally no&shy;
thing else but <term lang="la">Vigi&shy;
li&aelig;</term>, as Mr. Lloyd 
rightly judgeth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">30. [British] Gw&igrave;liay,</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">i&shy;
dem</term>: which ac&shy;
cording to the Welsh 
Syntax is sometimes 
<term lang="br">Wiliay</term>. Properly it 
signifies only Holy&shy;
days, and is doubt&shy;
less derived from 
the Latine word <term lang="la">Vi&shy;
gili&aelig;</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="foumart(n)">31. [English] A Fowmart,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="polecat(n)"> 
a Polecat. Martes 
is a noted Beast of 
this verminous kind, 

</page>
<page no="129">

desired for their 
Furs; whence per&shy;
chance the Polecat 
might be denomi&shy;
nated Foumart q. 
Foul mart from its 
stinking smell.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">31. [British] Phw'lbart,</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">i&shy;
dem</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dirdum(n)">32. [English] Durdom,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="noise(n)"> noise.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">35. [British] M&egrave;r,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="water(n)" lexeme="sallow tree(n)"> Water;
