Geese.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="seam(n)">37. [English] Saime,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="goose-grease(n)" lexeme="grease(n)"> which 
we pronounce 
sometimes Seame. 
It signifies not only 
Goose-grease, but 
in general any kind 
of Grease or Sewet 
or Oil, wherewith 
our Clothiers a&shy;
noint or besmear 
their Wool to make 
it run or draw out 
in Spinning. It is 
a general word in 
most Countries.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">37. [British] Saim,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="grease(n)"> Grease, 
of the same Foun&shy;
tain doubtless with 
the Latine word <term lang="la">Se&shy;
bum</term>. I should ra&shy;
ther think with the 
Hebrew <term lang="he">Shamen 
Pingu&egrave;do</term>. <term lang="la">Sevum</term>
not being a general

</page>
<page no="130">

word for Fat or 
Grease, but proper 
for Tallow or hard 
Fat.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="spoke(n)">38. [English] Spokes</form> <xpln lang="en"> of a 
Wheel.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">38. [British] Yspagay,</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> Legs, 
used also metapho&shy;
rically for the feet 
of a Stool.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="glaive(n)">39. [English] A Glaive;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="sword(n)" lexeme="bill(n)"> a 
Sword or Bill.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">39. [British] Gla&igrave;v,</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> a Bill, 
it is French word.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="riddle(n)">40. [English] A Riddle;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="sieve(n)" lexeme="riddle(n)"> a 
course Sieve. We 
make a difference 
between a Riddle &amp; 
a Sieve. A Riddle 
is of an oblong fi&shy;
gue, whereas a Sieve 
is round: and a 
Riddle is made of 
round Wickers 
placed longways 
one by another, 
whereas a Sieve is 
made of thin long 
Plates, as it were 
woven together, so 
that the holes of it 
are four-square.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="br">40. [British] Rhidilh,</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> i&shy;
dem.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="131">

<heading>
A Catalogue of North Country 
Words received from Mr. Tom&shy;
linson of Edmund Hall, a Cum&shy;
berland Gentleman, and com&shy;
municated to me by the same 
Mr. Edward Lloyd.
</heading>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="beck(n)">A Beck,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="rivulet(n)"> a Rivulet or small Brook. This 
Word is already entred among the 
Northern words; and noted to be common to 
the ancient Saxon, High and Low Dutch, 
and Danish. It is used not only in the North, 
but in some Southern and Western Coun&shy;
ties; and gives denomination  to some Towns, 
as Welbeck, Sandbeck, Troutbeck.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bourn(n)" lexeme="burn(n)">Bourn or Burn,</form> <xpln lang="en"> a Rivulet or Spring. 
This is also common to some Southern Coun&shy;
ties, and gives denomination to many Towns, 
as Sherburn, Milburn, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bourtree(n)">Bore-tree,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="elder-tree(n)"> Elder-tree: from the great 
pith in the younger branches which Chil&shy;
dren commonly bore out to make Pot&shy;
guns of them.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="132">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bracken(n)">Bracken,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="fern(n)" lexeme="brake(n)"> Fern. <term lang="la">ab</term> Angl. Break, be&shy;
cause when its moisture is dried up it is 
very brittle. A Brake is an instrument 
to break Flax with, of the same original. 
Break comes from the Saxon <term lang="oe">Brecan</term>. Brake 
Fern is a general word all England over; 
and better known in this Country [Essex] 
then Fern; indeed the only word in use a&shy;
mong the Vulgar, who understand not Fern. 
Bracken is but the plural of Brake, as Eyn 
of Ey, and Peasen of Pease. &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>
	
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="brent-brow(n)">Brent-brow,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="brow(n)"> a steep Hill, Metaph. The 
brow of a Hill, <term lang="la">Supercilium</term>, the edge or 
side of a Hill or Precipice.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="brock(n)">A Brock,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="badger(n)" lexeme="gray(n)"> a Badger. This is a word 
known in most Countries. The Ani&shy;
mal is trionymous, Badger, Brock or 
Gray.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="coop(n)">To Coop or Cowp.</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chaffer(v)" lexeme="exchange(v)" lexeme="boot(v)"> To chaffer or ex&shy;
change. It is a Low Dutch word. That 
which is given by the party which hath 
the worst Goods is called boot; as What 
Root will you give me between your old 
Yawd and my Filly? i.e. between your 
old Mare and my young one: <term lang="la">ab</term> AS. 
Bot reward or recompense. To boot is used 
frequently in the same sense all England over. 
Boot signifies profit, as in that impersonal 
Verb, It booteth not, It profiteth, helpeth 
or availeth not.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="133">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="copt-know(n)">Copt-know,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="copt(n)" lexeme="knoll(n)"> The top of a Hill rising 
like a Cone or Sugar-Loaf. Copt I 
conceive, comes from Caput, and Know 
or Knolle is the top of a Hill.</xpln>
<lemenote> "copt(n)" and "copt-know(n)" not found in OED. </lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cowdy(n)">A Cowdy,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="cow(n)"> a little Cow, a Scotch Runt 
without Horns, or else with very short 
ones, scarce exceeding a South-Country 
Veal in height:  So that the word is only a 
diminutive of Cow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en"><term lang="nb">A Creil,</term></form> <xpln lang="en"> a short, stubbed, dwarfish man. 
Northumberland.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="croft(n)">A Croft,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="close(n)"  lexeme="enclosure(n)"> a small Close or inclosure, at 
one end whereof a dwelling House with 
a Garth or Kitchin-Garden is usually 
placed: <term lang="la">ab</term> AS. <term lang="oe">Croft</term>, <term lang="la">Agellulus</term>. Croft 
for any small Field or inclosure in ge&shy;
neral, without any respect to a Mansion 
House, is common in all Counties of Eng&shy;
land.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cyphel(n)">Cyphel,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="houseleek(n)"> Houseleek.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dish-cradle(n)"><term lang="cu">A Dish-Cradle or Credle,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="cube(n)" lexeme="die(n)" lexeme="parallelepipedon(n)"> a wooden U&shy;
tensil for wooden Dishes, much in use in 
the North of England, made usually like 
a Cube or Die, and sometimes like a Pa&shy;
rallelipipedon, long Cube; or Cradle, 
Cumber.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="dike(n)" lexeme="dyke(n)">A Dike,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ditch(n)" lexeme="sough(n)"> a Ditch, this is only a variety 
of Dialect. Though it seems Dyke and 
Seugh or Sough are distinguished in the 
North, a Dyke being a Ditch to a dry

</page>
<page no="134">

Hedge, either of Trees or Earth, as in 
arable Lands, where the Ditch is usually 
dry all Summer; but a Sough a Ditch brim&shy;
ful of water, as in Meadows or Sow&shy;
brows are not above 1/2 yard in height. A 
Sough is a subterraneous vault or channel cut 
through a Hill to lay Coal Mines or any other 
Mine dry.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="doubler(n)">A Dubler or Doubler,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="platter(n)" lexeme="dish(n)"> a Platter or Dish. 
<term lang="la">Vox per magnam Anglia partem diffusa</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="draff(n)">Draffe,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="malt(n)"> the Grains of Malt, &agrave; Belg. 
<term lang="bl">Draf</term> <term lang="la">ejusdem significati</term>. This is a ge&shy;
neral word, signifying not only Grains, but 
Swill, as in those Proverbs, Draffe is good 
enough for Swine, and, The still Sow 
eates up all the Draffe.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="foumart(n)">A Fowmart,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="polecat(n)" lexeme="fitchet(n)"> a Polecat or Fitchet: Brit. 
<term lang="br">Ffwlbarth</term>. This is entred in the Col&shy;
lection.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gill(n)">A Gill,</form> <xpln lang="en"> a place hem'd in with two steep 
brows or banks, usually flourishing with 
Brushwood, a rivulet running between 
them. It's entred in the Collection.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="goose-cree(n)">A Geose or Grose cree;</form> <xpln lang="en"> a Hut to put 
Geese in.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gob(n)">A Gob,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gobble(v)" lexeme="gob(n)" lexeme="gobbet(n)"> an open or wide mouth. Hence 
to goble, to swallow greedily, or with 
open mouth. Gob in the South signifies a 
large morsel or bit, so we say a good Gob 
i.e. a good Segment or part. The diminu&shy;

</page>
<page no="135">

tive whereof is Gobbet; cut into gobbets, 
perchance from the Greek word <term lang="gk">&#x03F0;&#x03CC;&pi;&tau;&alpha;,
&#x03F0;&#x03CC;&mu;&mu;&alpha;</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gully(n)">A Gully;</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="knife(n)"> a large household Knife.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gavelock(n)">A Gavelock,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="iron crown(n)"> an iron Crown, <term lang="la">ab</term> AS. 
<term lang="oe">Gaveloc</term>. <term lang="la">catapulta, balista</term>. Already 
entred.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="heather(n)">Hadder,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="heath(n)" lexeme="ling(n)"> Heath or Ling.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hallan(n)">The Hollen,</form> <xpln lang="en"> is a wall about 2 1/2 yards 
high, used in dwelling Houses to secure 
the Family from the blasts of wind, rushing 
in when the heck is open, To this Wall 
on that side next to the Hearth is annex'd 
a Sconce or Skreen of Wood or Stone.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hen-balk(n)">Hen-bawks,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hen roost(n)"> A Hen Roost, from the 
Bawks of which it consists. v. Bawks.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="knur(n)">A Knor or Knurre,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="dwarfish man(n)"> a short stubbed 
dwarfish Man. Metaph. from a Knor, 
or Knot in a Tree. In the South we use 
the Diminutive Knurle in the same sense.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="coil of hay(n)"><term lang="nb">A Keil of Hay,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="cock of hay(n)"> a Cock of Hay.  
Northumberl.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="losset(n)">A Losset,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="voider(n)"> a large flat wooden dish not 
much unlike a Voider.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mouldwarp(n)">A Mould warp,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="mould(n)" lexeme="wort(v)"> a Mole, <term lang="oe">Mold</term> in the 
Saxon is dust, in English Mould is used for 
Earth, especially among Gardeners. 
<term lang="ld">Worpen</term> in Low Dutch is cast forth, 
whence to wort is to cast forth as a 
Mole or Hog doth. This is a word known

</page>
<page no="136">

all over England, though not in frequent 
use.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">A Mell,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beetle(n)" lexeme="door(n)" lexeme="byre(n)" lexeme="groop(n)" lexeme="fossula(n)"> a wooden sledge or Beetle, <term lang="la">ab</term>
AS, <term lang="oe">Mell</term>, Crux, from the exact resem&shy;
blance of the Head and Shaft (or handle) 
especially before the upper part of the 
Shaft is cut off, to a Cross. Hence Mel&shy;
deors (or Doors) a passage through a 
dwelling house. For in the North parts 
of England the Houses of those of the in&shy;
ferior sort have a passage through them 
with a Door or Heck on one side into the 
dwelling House, and another on the other 
side into the Byer, where they bind their 
Cows, Oxen, &amp;c. lengthways on each 
side. This Byer hath a Grupe, Groop or 
Fossula in the midst from the Door to 
the other end: so that the Fossula from the 
Door to the other end represents the 
Shaft of a Mell; or the streight Tree 
in a cross, and the passage though the 
House the Head or transvers Tree.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="purr(n)">A Porr,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="glazier(n)" lexeme="plumber(n)" lexeme="salamander(n)"> a  Glasier or Plummer, a Sala&shy;
mander.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pot-clip(n)">Pot-cleps,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pot-hook(n)"> Pot-hooks, from clip or clap, 
because they clap or catch hold of the 
Pot.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="rud(n)">Rud,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bloodstone(n)"> a sort of Blood-stone used in 
marking Sheep; from the red colour.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
<page no="137">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="riggald(n)">A Riggilt,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="ram(n)" lexeme="tup-hog(n)" lexeme="gimmer-hog(n)" lexeme="twinter(n)"> a Ram with one Stone. A 
Tup-Hog is a Ram of one year old: a 
Gimmer-hog, an Ewe of the same age. 
A twinter is a Hog two years old.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexe="roop(n)">A Roop,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hoarseness(n)"> a Hoarseness: &agrave; Cimbrico 
<term lang="ci">Hroop</term> <term lang="la">vel</term> <term lang="ci">Heroop</term>, <term lang="la">vociferatio</term>, by which it 
is frequently contracted.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="smithy(n)">Smidy,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="smithy coom(n)"> a Smiths Shop, whence Smidy&shy;
koom. Var. Dial.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="steg(n)">A Steg,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gander(n)"> a Gander.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="slam one(v)">To Slam one,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beat(v)" lexeme="cuff(v)" lexeme="slam(a)" lexeme="slim(a)"> to beat or cuffe one stre&shy;
nuously. A slam or slim Fellow is a 
skragged, tall, rawboned Fellow, the 
length of whose Arms gives him the 
advantage of strinking hard, and there&shy;
fore such are noted for fisty-cuffs; whence 
slam seems to be derived.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="snurl(n)">Snurles,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="nostril(n)"> Nostrils.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="sour-milk(n)">Sower-milk,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="butter-milk(n)"> Butter-milk. Sower from 
its long standing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="swang(n)">A Swang,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="tarn(n)" lexeme="lough(n)"> <term lang="la">locus paludosus</term>, or part of 
a Pasture overflow'd with water, not 
much unlike a Tarn or Lough, whence 
the Grass by the superfluity of an olea&shy;
ginous moisture degenerates into course 
Piles, which in Summer (most of the 
water being exhal'd) is so interwoven 
with thick mud and slime, and the Piles 
so long and top-heavy, that they embrace 
the surface of the Mud, and compose

</page>
<page no="138">

a verdure like that of a Meadow.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="swine-greun(n)">Swine greun,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="swine's snout(n)" lexeme="grin(v)"> a Swines snout, a Dan. 
and Island. <term lang="da">Graun</term> <term lang="la">Nasus, superius la&shy;
brum</term>. Whence our English word to grin, 
because in grinning the Muscles of the 
upper Lip are contracted.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="tab(n)">Tabs,</form> <xpln lang="en"> Childrens hanging sleeves, a 
Tab for a Shooe Latchet is already entred.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="thin-drink(n)">Thin-drink,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beer(n)"> small Beer, <term lang="la">Cerevisia tenu&shy;
is</term>, whence thin is derived. The Low 
Dutch use thick Beer for strong Beer; though 
to say the truth, that they call thick Beer is 
properly so, very thick and muddy.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="wad(n)"><term lang="cu">Wad,</term></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="black lead(n)"> Black lead, Cumberland. See 
Mr. Nicolsons Catalogue.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="walsh(a)">Walsh or Welsh,</form> <xpln lang="en"> strange, insipid, <term lang="la">ab</term> AS. 
<term lang="oe">Wealh</term>, <term lang="la">vel poti&ugrave;s Teutonico</term> Welsch 
strange: Welsh Potage, strange, insipid 
Potage.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="unlede(n)">Unleed or Unlead,</form> <xpln lang="en"> a general name for 
any crawling venomous creature; as a 
Toad, &amp;c. It's sometimes ascribed to 
Man, and then it denotes a sly wicked 
fellow, that in a manner creeps to do 
mischief, the very pest of Society. See 
Mr. Nicolsons Catalogue.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="whinnock(n)" lexeme="kit(n)">A Whinnock or Kit,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pail(n)"> a Pail to carry Milk 
in.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>
